And let him be careful that the position of the limb does not follow a pain. Because when he feels pain or injury in the position of the limb, then it is necessary that he changes himself to another position from which he does not feel pain.
And be careful with him so that his position is equal and straight; so that no twisting occurs in the limb when it is being restored.
The quality of the tightening of the broken limb is as I have told.
Know that the broken limbs are diversified according to their smallness, and the size of them, and their shape.
Those that are small, such as the arm, and finger, and foot, and similar ones; then it is necessary that there be a wrapping of soft and delicate cloth. But those that are large, such as the hip and leg; it is necessary that there be a wrapping wide and hard in them.
Because a wide bandage connects a large limb and tightens it uniformly from all sides with an equal tightening, so that no stain enters it.
Then begin after your amputation to place a suitable ointment on it, on soft wool at the site of the fracture itself. Then begin to roll the ligament over the location of the fracture with three or four rolls, according as the limb deserves; and tighten your hand slightly with the ligament.
Then go with it to the upper part from the location of the fracture, and tighten it less than your previous binding that you bound the fractured place. Then stretch the roll from the location of the fracture slightly, and soften the binding gradually until you take from a healthy place a large amount.
Then take another linen cloth, and roll it again over the fractured place with rolls. Then go with the roll to the lower part from the fracture. And let your operation be in binding and softening of the roll, according to what we said in the upper roll.
Then place between the rolls from soft wool or cloth equal to the curvature of the fracture if there is any curvature; and if not, do not put anything there.
Then roll over another linen cloth. Then place stars evenly over these rolls immediately, if there is no swelling or hot abscess.
If there is swelling or an abscess, then place over it what sits under the abscess, and remove the swelling; and leave it for some days. Then bind over them the stars.
The stars should be made from the middle of a wide reed, shaved and well-formed; or they may be made of wood from which cribra are made, such as pine, elder, or palm branches, or calingi; and let the width of all the stars be according to this form and the same figure.
However, the star that is placed over the affected limb should be a little thicker and wider than the other stars; and the length of the star should be according to what is appropriate for the member in size and thickness.
Then bind over the Star with another plaster, according to the first binding. Then bind properly with threads, as we said regarding binding; and this is so that your binding over the site of the fracture is stronger than the fracture itself; and the farther away from the fracture the binding is, the less it should be.
The threads should be of medium thickness and fineness, and made of soft linen.
Because if the threads are thick, as I have seen in the operation of fools binding with thick cords of twisted linen, there is a great error; for the binding falls away from them due to their poor quality.
And thin threads do not fit again; because you will not obtain with the binding what you wish.
It is not appropriate that there be less than a finger's width between one star (stitch) and another.
If the patient is injured by the extremities of the stitches after binding in healthy places: then place beneath them with soft wool or camel's hair until nothing is injured from it.
But when there is a fracture with a wound, and the break occurs in the skin, I will come back with regard to this matter in Chapter 2, specifically.
And know that not every fractured member should be bound with stitches from the first day. Because when a member is large, it is not necessary to place stitches on top of it unless five days, or seven, or more have passed, according to your safety from the risk of hot abscess.
This is the method of plasters, with which the ancients restored fractures and dislocations.
Â§ Common Plaster Method summarized, restoring a fracture, suitable for multiple temperaments, particularly women and children; because it is from that which heat does not prevail, nor cold.
And it is to receive from the flour mill dust, and it is the kernel of wheat that hangs on the walls of the mill during the movement of the millstone; and make it thus, except that you sieve it with egg white; and place its mixture not thick, nor thin. Then use (it).
Another emplastrum method, for healing a fracture, and dislocation, and twisting. Prepare almess, laudanum, acacia, enula, and mugath; squeeze, from all of them an equal amount of juice, 10 parts of myrrh, 5 parts of each aloes, and 20 parts of alethil should be ground and sieved, and mixed with water alethil, or with egg white if the covering is heated for the weak. Then use this emplastrum. For it is a good mixture, it heals broken bones quickly, and benefits many people.
Another emplastrum method, which also helps with fractures and twisting. Prepare mugath, mess, white alteae, all in an equal amount of 10 parts of myrrh, 5 parts each of both aloes, 6 parts of acacia, and 20 parts of Armenian pounded bulbs should be ground with much pressure, sieved, and mixed with water or egg white; administer them.
Another emplastrum method for joints and separated bones from their places; it relieves pain that comes to them, and fissure of the bone, and fracture. Take sheared wool, immerse it in vinegar, boiled oil; place it on the location.
And in this emplastrum there is no virtue of healing, but it is good for the relief of hot boils; and it drives out pains properly.
Method of Empalisting, which restores a broken bone. Take leaves from the fig tree of deafness and leaves from the wild papaver (opium poppy) tree; grind them all together and make an ointment from both.
Method of quick emplasting for the restoration of a broken bone and when you wish to dissolve a remaining boil. Take equal parts of root of althea (marsh-mallow), chamomile, flowers of violets, and herb flour; grind all together and make a paste with wine if the member is not heated.
If it is heated (the affected area), then make the paste with warm coriander water or with water and use it.
Another stronger method for the dissolution (of a boil), which is more powerful than the first, when a hard boil appears during the restoration of a member. Take equal parts of root of althea (marsh-mallow), linseed, balustium (balm of Gilead), melilotus, samsucci (white poplar), flowers of violets and chamomile; grind all together and make a paste with sweet water, salix water or wine. Apply this (paste) according to the warmth of the member and the calmness of its heat.
Hippocrates, however, did not say in his book that it should be placed on a broken member during its restoration except for a plaster made from beeswax and oil; no other (substance); and he said that it should be medium between thickness and subtlety.
Galenus, however, saw fit that such materials should be placed on a broken member during its restoration as those in which there is an absorption of heat, such as myrrh, aloes and frankincense, and similar ones from what we have mentioned.
Method of plaster application for wound and pain. Mix ground figs, chickpeas, human hair cuttings or bird feathers, alum and equal parts of salt. Grind and sieve them, then make a plaster from this mixture.
The amount that should remain bound depends on whether pain does not occur, nor itchiness, nor is the broken bone moved from its place. Then do not dissolve it for many days. If there is severe itchiness, disturbing pain or swelling at the site, then hurry and dissolve it within an hour, remove the plaster from it: take a soft cloth or seawater-soaked sponge, moisten it with warm water, wash the area until the itchiness subsides and the pain is relieved.
If there is severe itchiness, disturbing pain or swelling at the site, then hurry and dissolve it within an hour, remove the plaster from it: take a soft cloth or seawater-soaked sponge, moisten it with warm water, wash the area until the itchiness subsides and the pain is relieved. Then let the limb rest for one hour. Then place on it a piece of soft, vinegar- and oil-soaked wool or rose oil: tie it over it at night until you are safe from a hot abscess, the swelling of the limb subsides, and the pain is relieved.
Then repeatedly apply fine binding and small plaster: do not bind it with your initial tight binding or delicate bandages until it is healed.
If you see that the abscess, redness, pain, swelling and all other symptoms have been completely removed, and if you need plaster and binding, then do as before equally.
If there is no symptom in the limb that we mentioned before, then do not dissolve it, unless after three days, four, five or seven. And when it is released after twenty days, according to your observation from the dissolution of the limb, as we said, until the fracture adheres and the coagulation of flesh grows on it: also add more binding in your binding than your first binding, and also increase the amount of weak food, according to the reminder of its presence.
If you see that the place of the fracture is already drying out and shrinking too much: then you know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. Pour warm embrocation onto it at every juncture where you are dissolving it, and let it be for all three days, and alum tighten it slightly.
By this procedure, nourishment flows to the limb and it heals quickly.
But what foolish restorers do, replacing the fracture of a limb by another method, when it should not be replaced at all according to what is necessary, and it is replaced crookedly, is an error from their procedure, and great fear: and if it had been correct, they would have said it in their ancient books, and operated with it.
And I did not see any trace of theirs at all in that matter, and it is more correct to not do that.
[Section] On the making of an injury in the head.
Species of fractures are diverse, and their shapes numerous: and causes of it are distinguished.
From a fracture is one that occurs from a blow with a sword, and the blow removes the bone completely to where it reaches the pith, which is under the bone, just as an ax does in wood; and because of this, this type of fracture is called Axial Fracture.
Or a sword cuts something of the bone and cuts its face but does not cut through to the end of it; and this type of fracture is called Calcan, that is, Absolute Lever Fracture.
And there is a wound of two fractures, either large or small.
And from a fracture is one that is crushing, or grinding; and the cause of it is a blow with a stone, or a fall upon a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also penetrating, which already approaches the flesh that is under the bone; or it is on the face of the bone; and this type of fracture has a wound, either small or large.
And from a fracture is one that is hidden in the bone at its subtlety (the tip) of a hair; and it is a small crack, and because of this, this type of fracture is called Hairline Fracture.
And from these (fractures) is a fracture that occurs from a fall or blow of a stone and similar things, which make the layer of bone to enter inside and make a hollow place; as happens with brass caldrons when they are struck and part of them enters inside.
Indeed, it most frequently occurs in the bones of moist heads, such as those of children's skulls.
And all these types of fractures are set separately, and I will return with a reminder of this (treatise) to its place.
We know all these types of fractures through examination upon them, and investigation of their nature with probing, and removal of the rotten flesh that is on them.
The species of Capillaris is known by this, that it appears on the face and is scraped off and sewn over with an ointment; for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of a fracture, consider first the condition of the patient; then if you see from his conditions that it indicates a manifest sign of fear, such as cholera vomiting, stretching, loss of intellect, absence of speech, fainting, acute fever, eye discharge, redness of eyes and similar conditions in multiple disorders, without a doubt it will not heal; but if you see non-terrifying conditions and hope for recovery, then begin the treatment.
If someone comes to you wounded first with a wound, and this is in winter days, then it is necessary that you strive for the removal of the bone before the 14th day under any circumstances.
And if it is in summer days, then it is necessary to hurry and remove the bone before the 7th day, before what is under the bone from fat corrupts, and such conditions occur as we have mentioned.
If the fracture of a bone has already reached the fat touching the brain and is accompanied by grinding and pulling, then it is necessary to cut the grinded and smoothed part, as I have told you.
And it is necessary to shave the head of the wounded person; and to examine the face according to whatever method possible for you; and according to the shape of the wound, and what is light on the patient.
If blood flow or hot abscess occurs to you while examining the bone, then oppose it with what is necessary. And it is that you fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil; and make the wound rest until the abscess settles, and be careful of bleeding. Then begin with the treatment of the bone, and its removal.
And that is done according to one of two methods.
One method is to cut the bone with a sharp knife; and this is its form.
Then administer another knife wider than the first, somewhat; and this is its form.
Then administer yet another knife even wider than the second one.
And the second method is that you have a number of different knives; some of which are wider than others; and some shorter than others; and they should be at the ends of sharpness, and made of iron or steel; and use them with ease in striking the knife, so as not to hit the head and hurt it.
If the bone is hard, then it is necessary to bore around its circumference before you administer the knife, with trephines that are not too deep; and they are not called thus unless because they do not penetrate through the bone to what lies beyond it; therefore the tip of the trephine is rounded above that which is beneath the head, sharp below, like an edge, and a small circle prevents submerging and penetrating the thickness of the bone.
And it is suitable for you to take a number of these trephines, one of which should correspond to the thickness of the bone, until the present bone has all the trephines; whose sharp edges should be of a quantity in length and width, according to the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three kinds of trephines or trepans.
The nature of the perforation on the bone fracture is as follows: you should place the trephine upon the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has already been penetrated. Then change the trephine to another place and place there the size of a fingertip within every hole, or about that much.
Then cut with the chisels what is between each hole in the bone, and make that more delicate than possible on top of the edge of smoothness, as we said, until you root out the bone, either with your hand or with some other tools which you have prepared for it: such as tenaliae and subtle forpices.
And it is necessary that you take all caution so that the trephine or chisel does not touch any part of the skull.
When you are perforating the bone and it is separated from the skull, if it is continuous with it and you extract it, it is necessary that you file down, and even out the remaining part on the bone with another similar chisel. Nevertheless, it should be thin and delicate more than other chisels.
If some part of the small bones remains and the cartilages: then take them with ease, with whatever tools are at hand. Then care for the wound after that with linen and ointments as we said.
Another method of the operation is easy, completely far from fear: which Galen said and praised it greatly: this is his speech. It is necessary for you first to begin to remove the bone from the place where the fracture is more violent and clear: until when you have removed that part, and placed under it the end of this concave chisel, whose shape this is.
And the concave part of the lens, not cutting anything: and the sharp part of it extending in two sides of it, as you see: so that the concave part of the lens is attached to the siphoid, with its sharp edge incident on the bone.
Then strike upon the Incisorium from one side with a small hammer, until you cut through the bone easily, as is customary: and be careful that no cloth happens to fall into it, which might happen accidentally, which you fear entirely.
And if the physician were not more foolish than men, nor ignorant of it, nor asleep, then if anything remained attached to the bone from the cloth in some places, he would cut it off along with the edge of the incisorium lenticularis itself, and remove it easily. It is freed from it without harm, and without fear.
If the bone fracture does not penetrate to the cloth, and that which is cut off from the bone is its face, and coarseness and subtle spicules remain in it: then it is necessary that you smooth out that roughness, and grind out those spicules with finer rasps, which you have prepared in various amounts, so that it is possible for you to apply them more conveniently and correctly at each place according to the operation, and the fractured bone, and its figure. And it is necessary that you administer it according to your own measure, first grinding the bone more widely with those rasps. Then use a finer one after it.
And it is necessary that you administer it according to your own measure, first grinding the bone more widely with those rasps. Then use a finer one after it. And do not cease to do that continuously until you reach administering it more thinly and finer of them all.
In each of the remaining small fissures and fine fractures, it is necessary to administer care according to that which leads to its correction.
And it is a matter that cannot be hidden over all, to which in this art every study applies, and it stands on what we have written and shown regarding large fractures.
If the membrane is found to be retained when you are removing bone: then it is necessary that you take a linen cloth, according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the opening of the wound. Then take another cloth, either doubled or tripled, and immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it on top of the first one; make it as easy as possible to do so without burdening the wound.
Then administer it on top of the broad ligament, and do not tie it unless for the amount that holds the wound only; and leave it with this care for one day or two days, until you are assured of being free from hot inflammation. Then dissolve it and administer the following remedies with which excessive dryness is needed: such as the root of the lily, and grain of wheat, and pepper, and aristolochia, and similar ones; and it is to be made from these remedies a powder that is powdered over the wound like dry things.
And universally administer all medicine with the property of purging and not biting (or harming). And be careful according to your strength (ability), so that the wound is clean during your treatment; neither let it be dirty with ointment nor filthy.
And be careful according to your strength (ability), so that the wound is clean during your treatment; neither let it be dirty with ointment nor filthy. Do not allow the pus to accumulate in it at all, since when pus collects on a cerebral membrane, it corrodes it and rots it, and afterwards a great evil happens after that; and this has already happened in the head wound, when the bone is being cleansed from it, especially when care of it is neglected, blackness appears on its surface.
Consider then, if afflictions occur to the sick as we have said, know that it is certainly dangerous (or fatal).
If the swelling does not occur, unless it is caused by the medicine placed upon him, and it is in the power of that medicine to cause the swelling: then it is necessary for you to take one part from honey and three parts from rose oil, and anoint with them a cloth. Then place him on a sow's bladder: afterwards, take care of him with medicinal substances that are appropriate, until he is cured.
On the Restoration of the Nose when it is Broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except for its two superior parts together or one of them: this is because there are two bones in it; since its lower part is cartilaginous, it does not break and no other damage occurs to it except abrasion, twisting, and deviation.
If one of the two parts is broken, then it is necessary for you to insert a small finger into the nostril and align the fracture with your index and thumb fingers from outside until the nose returns to its natural shape; and this should be done skillfully and easily. And beware that during your procedure you do not cause pain to the sick person.
If the fracture is in the superior part of the nose and the finger does not reach it: then the radius must be aligned at the end where there is a small bulge.
If the fracture is in two parts, then do the same: and hasten with its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day in a warm room of sweating patients.
Then insert a strip of linen from a flax cloth into the end of the nose if the fracture is in one part; or insert two strips if the fracture is in two parts: and let there be a bulge of sufficient size on the linen to fill the nostril.
And some of the ancient restorers said that linseed should be poured into butter and exchanged daily. That does not seem right to me, on the contrary, it should be that linseed is poured into butter made from cow's milk, mixed with flour milled powder. Then let the linseed go until the bone hardens and the cartilage becomes calloused.
And sometimes a goose feather is inserted into the place of the linseed, after it has been covered with soft cloth; and this causes the fracture in the nose to be held more firmly, and prevents the weak from being prevented from breathing.
And this is not necessary. If you wish, do it; if you wish, do it with linseed.
If the nose again occurs while you are operating on a boil, fill the nose with hot plasters made of alum or pitch soaked in rose oil or with a little jalap.
But if it does not occur that the boil is hot, then it is necessary to fill it with plasters made of wheat bran and incense powder mixed with butter from cow's milk. Then place a soft wad on top of it, and do not tie the nose with anything at all.
If the bones of the nose are broken in a small fracture or ground, then it is necessary to cut through them and extract them with instruments that are suitable for them. Then take care of it with it until it heals and hardens with suitable plasters or ointments.
If a wound occurs between the nose, then it is necessary to treat it with linseed; and administer a lead tube until it heals.
Chapter on the restoration of the lower jaw when it breaks apart.
When the lower jaw is broken and the fracture is not accompanied by a wound: observe then if the fracture is only on the outside and it does not break into two, and bends towards the inside: then its recognition is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side of the jaw, you should insert your left index finger into the weakened bone: similarly, if the fracture is in the left jaw, then insert your right index finger: and press with it the swelling of the fracture towards the outside with ease using your other hand from the inner part of the bone properly, and push it equally.
If the fracture of the jaw has already separated into two parts, then it is necessary to administer traction from both sides, according to rectitude: until a possible alignment can be achieved.
If however movement or displacement of the teeth has already occurred, or if they are broken: then bind them with a thread of gold, silver, or silk. Then place linen cloth over the broken jaw. Then place over it a double cloth and place on top of the cloth a large cross made properly or an equal piece of leather soles in length of the jaw.
Then bind it above as you prepare the binding for it and its application is suitable until it is not destroyed: and tell the patient to remain calm and at peace: and place mild soft food for him.
If you think that something has changed in shape by some means: then go quickly and dissolve it on the third day. Then correct what has changed from it, and apply a plaster made of milled flour with egg white or with wheat flour, after you have removed the linen cloth: and place soft wool over the plaster.
While that adhering plaster remains on it, and the bone arrangement does not change, leave it undisturbed until it heals; or suture the fracture. This fracture is frequently sutured in three weeks.
If a warm abscess should occur meanwhile, then administer what I said several times for its alleviation until the abscess is removed.
And if there is a fracture with a wound, be aware. If already the bone fragment is separating out, or from a wound bed: then be skillful in this matter, to remove those fragments of the wound bed together with what fits with you in their removal using instruments.
If, however, the opening of the wound is narrow, then enlarge it with a leech according to your need.
Then when you have removed the wound bed and nothing remains of it, or if the opening of the wound is wide: and if not, place on top of it ointments that are suitable for this, and let it heal until healed.
Chapter on Restoring a Fork (when it breaks).
The fork breaks most often from the front part near the shoulder, and its fracture occurs according to one of three modes.
Or it breaks apart into two parts, apart from the presence of wound bed; and this is easier to restore.
"Likewise, that which happens in ossify satagia: and it is more difficult for restoration.
"Likewise, that which is a fracture with a wound.
"And the operation in it when there is a fracture without a wound, is to have two assistants present, so that one holds the support for the broken furcula, and the other extends the neck towards the opposite side.
"Then make an equal fracture with your fingers until it takes the desired shape; there should be neither protrusion nor hollow in it.
"If you need more extension, then it is necessary to place a ball under the weak person's head made of cloth or wool; and let its size be according to your need; and stretch, lift, and compress the ball with your hand until an equal fracture is achieved, as required.
"If you cannot pull out the extremity of the furcula because it has reached deep inside; then the weak person should lie on his neck; and place a puluinar medium under his shoulder in size, and press an assistant's shoulder towards the bottom, until the bone of the furcula that is deep inside is lifted upwards; and then correct the fracture and make it equal with your fingers.
"If you feel a swelling from the furcula and it moves; then you should press on it and extract the swelling with ease.
"If it remains in the bone, then use your drill bit in its incision, after you have placed a saving instrument under the furcula; and the tool is made of wood or iron. "And this is its form.
"It resembles a spiral shell without hollow.
"And let its width be according to the need for it from the size and part of the bone. The length [of the instrument] is also as much as possible for you for the operation.
It has two ends, as you see: and one end should be wide, and the other narrow.
If the wound opening that you cut when extracting the splinter of bone is wide, and it gapes, remove the hot cautery; then bring together two lips of the wound with stitches.
If the wound opening is small, or if you fear hot cautery; then fill the wound with cloths and lint according to the size of the wound incision.
If hot cautery occurs, then soak the cloth in rose oil, vinegar and wine and place it on top of it.
But the binding of the bone without a wound and without cutting is to place plaster made from milled flour with egg white on it. And place a soft woolen cloth over it. Then place a sphere underneath if you need it. Then take the hemata, which is a roll that the Saracens hold, it should be very long; and its width should be one palm or approximately this much. Then place two lints on top of the woolen cloth and plaster. Then take a needle made from thin wood, whose width is three fingers, and similarly long. Then thread the needle with the lint. Then sew the cloth at the place of the hemata that falls on the break and location. Then place the hemata on the break as they are rolled, and sew it over his neck, and under his healthy side, and under his weak side: and bring it over all parts, and so that the binding holds the break securely; and this is what will not be hidden from you, and entirely, so that the needle does not remove the broken bone. It is necessary to examine the sick person every day. Whenever the ligament becomes soft and you see the knot moving, then correct it, and tighten the ligament.
Then place the sick person's sleep on his back, and put a small awl under his sleep near his side, so that when he is raised up, help comes to him from his side. Raise then the fracture of the collarbone through the elevation of the shoulder.
Or bind his arm to his neck; and do not loosen the bandage unless it occurs at the place occurring from itch or abscess, until twelve days.
Then renew the plaster if you see his face toward that (it). And add to the incision, and let it be, until it is healed and coagulated.
The fracture of the collarbone is tightened and supported for as many as forty days; and sometimes it happens to some people in less (time).
Chapter on the Restoration of a Fractured Spatula.
A spatula rarely breaks, and when it does, it is usually at a wide place; and the edges are the only ones that break from it.
When it breaks at that place, and breaks in the middle of it, and is not known unless by touch; then according to its shape, prepare the treatment for the fracture. And bring it back to its natural shape in every way possible to you.
Then put powder of millstone with egg white and soft wool on top, and place a small piece of double cloth on it. Then put a wide knot made from thin wood of the same size as the spatula or slightly larger on top.
If there is a depression under a star in any spatula's handle, then equal the depression with soft wool until the star descends equally.
Then roll over it with long alchemilla thread, using a suitable tightness and the firmness of the star, until it is not moved from its place.
And consider the suture in every day. Whenever the suture is softened, tighten it and equalize the star if it moves from its place: let the weak lie on the side of the healthy.
And indeed the spatula is restored within twenty days or thirty days.
When this number (20 or 30) is complete, then loosen the suture: and you will be safe. For it is from bones whose fracture is not feared nor their solution.
If it proceeds from a bone fragment and pierces beneath the skin, then cut above it and remove it. And do as we have said in the fracture of a clavicle from the application of hot compresses if anything happens from it.
Chapter on the restoration of a breast fracture.
The breast is sometimes broken in the middle and it rarely happens. Its extremities, however, are much more often broken and crushed due to their accidents.
When the middle of it is broken, which inclines towards the lower parts, and there occurs a violent pain, difficulty in breathing, and coughing; and perhaps blood vomiting occurs, and a depression is revealed in the fractured bone, and it is not hidden from sensation, its restoration is that the weak lies on its back: and place pulvinar between its spatulas.
Then press the humeri of it and join the ribs from both sides or flanks with hands: and compress in equal alignment of it, as much as possible for that and it agrees, until the shape of the bone returns, as it should be.
Then place on top of it (the wound) an ointment and a stopper (bandage), and on top of that place a small wooden peg from subtle wood such as willow or boxwood, and similar ones in a thin form, after you have wrapped it in cloth. Then bind it (the fracture) with a bandage so that it is not moved: and go around it according to its roundness to the back several times, and tighten it appropriately with a splint.
Then consider the bandage in every hour, and whenever it softens, tighten it: and if its loosening is caused by itching, corrosion that occurs at the place, or pain, or abscess: then hurry up, and unbind it, remove the ointment, and correct what has occurred with the method of its treatment.
Then apply the ointment back to the wound if you see it coming off: and attach a splint until it heals.
Chapter on the Restoration of Ribs when they are Broken.
Know that the break of ribs does not occur except in large areas which follow the back and from their extremities on the front side. And it does not happen to them because of rubbing, but rather because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of this (the broken rib) is not hidden from the sensation when it is investigated with fingers.
And the restoration of this (the broken rib) is done so that it is aligned with fingers according to the possible method until it is in its original shape as needed. Then apply ointment and bind the fractured bone with a splint if necessary.
If the break of ribs is towards the interior: then a severe pain occurs for the sick, and puncture, like that which occurs in pleurisy; because the bone pierces the lining, and even to him there occurs difficulty in breathing, cough, and blood vomiting many times; and this is difficult to cure.
And the ancients have been skilled in this with many instruments: and among them are those who say, to place foods for the sick that generate inflammation and windiness, so that the stomach is inflamed, and extended, and pushed outward towards the outside.
We however abhor, that a cause for making a hot abscess should occur, unless it happens. If it has already happened, then add (something) to it.
And some of them said: place a windy substance on the spot, then suck it out with force, and it is more similar to reasoning. Nevertheless, it is feared that windy superfluities are attracted to the spot due to the position of weakness.
And some of them said: the spot should be covered with wool that has already been soaked in hot oil; and lice should be placed between the ribs until it is filled; so that the bandage is equal when it is folded according to its roundness.
Then the sick person will be treated with a treatment of pleurisy from food and medicine.
If something powerful constricts the sick person, more than he can tolerate, and wounds his flesh with a sharp point or puncture, and we fear over him; then it is necessary that we cut open the spot and reveal the broken rib. Then we place on it an instrument that conserves the sifac (a tool used for breaking bones), whose memory has gone before; and we cut the bone easily, and cut it ourselves.
Then we bring together two lips of the wound, if it is large with suture; and treat it ourselves with plasters, until it heals.
If a hot abscess occurs meanwhile; then run, and put lice in rose oil, and place it on the spot; and treat the sick person with whatever is suitable for an abscess.
The patient should also lie on his side regarding the side he sleeps on, until it is healed.
On the restoration of a fracture of the spine and neck.
Fracture sometimes occurs in the bones of the neck, and it rarely happens to them that there is rubbing: and similarly with the spondylus of the back. Whenever such a thing happens to someone, and one wishes to know whether it can be cured or not: then consider it carefully. If you see his hands already softened, stunned and dead; and he cannot move them, nor extend them, nor contract them; and when you press them with your fingers and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it or any pain there: then you know that it is incurable, and in most cases it is mortal.
If he moves them and feels the pressure with his fingers and the prick: then you know that the hump of the bone is sound, and that what is weak will be cured with treatment.
If a similar thing happens to the spondylus of the back, and you wish to know whether it can be cured or not: then consider his feet; then if you see that they are softened, and that the same thing has happened as in the hands; when he lies on his back, flatulence and hunger come out without his will; when he lies on his stomach, hunger comes out without his will; when he tries to defecate, he cannot: then you know it is mortal. Therefore, no effort should be made in its treatment.
But if nothing like that happens to him, the condition is less severe.
And the treatment for what happens in that condition is to apply hot poultice; so place warm oil on the rubbed spondylus: or with the poultice of goose fat.
Place it on top of that frequently throughout the day until the hot poultice settles down; then place a plaster there, one that strengthens, dries and binds, and bind it with a bandage: and tell the patient to rest in peace and quiet. He shall not sleep on the part where he found pain until it is healed.
If it happens near the fracture of the bone sathaiatum, or something which is already separated from it: then it is necessary that you excise above the skin and remove it. Then gather together two edges of the wound if it is large, with suture. Then treat him with some ointment of the incarnation until it is healed.
If the last bone of alhausos is broken and it is the end of the tail: then it is necessary to introduce your left thumb into the anus, and with your right hand break the broken bone equally, according as possible, and with a second correction.
Then place him on his back and remove the bone: then treat the wound as before with the preceding care until it is healed.
Chapter on the restoration of ankle.
Chapter 11.
Rarely are the bones of the ankles broken. If they are broken, then the fracture is not except for comminution at their extremities; and they are cut in length and decline towards the inside; and it happens that a weak pain occurs at the location, and puncture; and the leg which is broken becomes paralyzed.
And the restoration of it is that you stretch out your hand over him until you know the shape of the fracture.
If the fracture is only at its extremities, then align that fracture according to the correction you are prepared with, until it resembles its natural shape.
If the fracture is in length or already declines towards the inside: then make the weak one lie on his stomach until you are prepared for the restoration of that fracture. When you align it (the bone), place an ointment on it; then place a pin made of wood or hide over it, and bind the bandage on it so that you do not fear the displacement of the fracture or the removal of the pin.
Apply an equal concavity to those [things] that fill it, until it is seized by a ligature according to equality; and command the sick person to sleep on his back or on his healthy side.
If however it happens to be a hot abscess: then cease from its extension and its restoration until the hot abscess settles; and place upon what is sitting the hot abscess, according to what has preceded, then return to its restoration; and bind it as necessary.
If however a fracture occurs in bone, or it is crushed, extract it.
Chapter on the restoration of fractures, Aid.
Aid is that which extends from the elbow to the head of the spatula.
If it therefore breaks, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One is: that you take a flat board of medium thickness, according to this shape which follows.
And bind two cords at its two ends.
Then it is suspended from a raised place; and the sick person sits on a chair. Then throw the broken arm over the board so that it reaches its hinge, where a notch is attached to the middle of the board.
Then suspend something heavy above it so that the servant extends towards the lower parts. The physician then sets the fracture with his hands until it returns to its proper position.
And another method is to lay the patient on his back and hang the hand from his neck with a rope. Then command two servants to hold one of them above the fracture with their hands and for the other to hold below it and each should extend to their respective sides.
And if you wish a stronger extension, then bind under the fracture with rope and above it with another rope and let each of the servants stretch to their respective parts.
If the fracture is near the end of the upper arm, then a rope should be placed under the elbow and another one below the fracture towards the forearm.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow, then a rope should be placed at that place and above the elbow.
Then set the fracture easily and without strain until it is in its proper position and applied well. Then bind it if no warm abscess occurs.
But if a warm abscess occurs, then release the binding until the seventh day. Place on it soft, scoured linen soaked in vinegar and rose oil until the abscess settles down. Then bind it and the method of binding is to place a plaster over the fracture.
Then apply a new cloth wrap over the plaster. Then raise the arm to support it and place your open hand on the shoulder.
Place a cloth and bind the supporting arm so that the arm remains in place like a star, unless it prevents you from moving or anything breaks off from the fractured bone.
If you fear that something might change from a broken bone, then use a larger and stronger awl: it is to place the awl over the fracture itself which is wider and stronger than other awls. Over every awl, place its width equal to a finger: and let the length of the awls be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers from all sides.
Then tie over the awls the ligature that I said at the beginning of this chapter.
And it is to be your ligature over the fracture site stronger. As the fracture lengthens, let the ligature become smaller.
If you see the position of the awls and ligature, as we have said, at the time when you restore the limb immediately, then do so: and if you fear a hot abscess, then release the ligature and awls until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then consider the ligature for three days, so that itchiness, swelling, or food not reaching the limb due to the tightness of the bandage does not occur. Correct it all, as we have said.
But if you are confident that nothing will happen from it: then do not release the ligature, unless after three days or more.
The sick person should lie on his back and place his hand on his stomach: and put under him a wool-filled cushion evenly.
Consider at all hours of the night or day that the figure of the broken limb is not loosened, or the ligature softened. Correct it all with your power.
Place the food for the sick person according to the order we have given, so that the food is subtle at first, until the bone begins to bind: then it is necessary for the food to be thickened.
In custom for assistance and of the leg, it should be bound for forty days: then therefore it is necessary, that it should be loosened, and a bath administered, and it should be treated with ointments which correct it.
If the break is foul, scrape it: then do not loosen the bandage and splints from it for fifty days, and until two months.
Chapter on the restoration of the broken arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called the two fore-arm bones.
One of them is small: and that is the one which follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is placed below the small one from beneath.
Therefore perhaps only the larger fore-arm bone breaks, or perhaps both break.
When therefore the smaller fore-arm bone above breaks, then the restoration of it is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the lower fore-arm bone breaks, the breakage of it is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And that is worse, when both bones break together.
If the bone that breaks is the smaller fore-arm bone above, then it is fitting for the doctor at its restoration, to set a smaller extension with ease, until it becomes equal (to the other part).
But if the larger fore-arm bone is the one that broke, then it is necessary to set a strong extension.
And it is necessary to set the shape of the hand at its restoration, and the extension of it extended beyond the wrist. The thumb of the hand should be higher than all the fingers above it: and the lower joint (of the thumb) should be higher than all the joints or the rest: and the wrist should be level with the weak one sitting squarely upon itself, and the wrist should be in front of it, or directly under it, at a height so that the weak one does not labor.
Then, the servant shall extend the arm from the lower part, either with his hand, or with a ligature. And another servant shall stretch [it] similarly from above.
Then the physician shall equalize the bone, until he reduces it to the best shape possible.
If there are shards in a broken bone, then try to return every fragment to its place using your strength.
If it appears that a shard has been separated and punctures the skin, and there is no hope for its restoration: then seal over it, and remove it as we have said in the previous [instructions].
If there is a fracture with an open wound, I have already made a special chapter for that, so follow its treatment from there.
If a hot abscess occurs at the beginning of your treatment, then take a cloth soaked with wax made from rose oil, white wax, and be in between thickness and subtlety; tie the cloth over it with a thin ligature until the abscess subsides; then remove the wax, and place an ointment prepared from flour mill powder and egg white. Then place the trepan (or trephine) over it, and let the trepan placed on the fracture be slightly wider and stronger.
And know that the number of trepans for the arm is six in multiple arrangements; whether it is a fracture in one hole or two holes simultaneously.
Then place a stronger and more intense ligature over the location of the fracture, and add some extra tension to the ligature above or below the fracture; or place a softer ligature slightly closer to the fracture, according to what was remembered in the beginning of the chapter.
And let the bandages wrapped around the fracture be made of soft, moist fabric; and they should not be hard or rough. And let the thread with which it is tied be made from linen, of medium thickness between subtle and coarse, as we have said.
Consider the limb and the ligament after days. If something happens for which correction is necessary, such as itching that occurs in a limb: then it is necessary, to anoint the limb with embroces and warm water until the itching ceases, and then let the limb alone until it has ceased, and then repeat the binding.
If the binding has already been softened and the bone has already been removed, and similar cases: then correct it completely with your skill, and consider it again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb due to an excess of binding, then it is necessary to soften it a little and let it alone for some days until it reaches its nourishment, and then bind it again.
But if nothing of what we have said happens to the sick one, then it is not necessary to loosen it unless after twenty days, or about this much.
Then the hand of the sick one should be suspended to his neck. And let his neck be kept straight and guarded with your skill against excessive movements. Make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty or thirty-two days; and it may be restored within twenty-eight days. Everything depends on the temperaments of the body and the qualities of strength.
Chapter on the Restoration of Fractures (Hand).
Know that fracture rarely occurs in shaving hands or tying fingers. And it does not happen to him unless he is rubbed many times.
When there is a fracture of the foot or rubbing: then the sick one should sit on a square seat and a chair be placed opposite him at an equal height. Then place his hand upon her extended [surface].
The assistant should then extend the broken bones and the physician should equalize them until they are united with a good union.
Then it is necessary that he places ointment and wool, unless there happens to be a hot abscess. Then place the wooden probe on it according to the size of the location; and you have already wound it up in linen cloth.
If the break is in the lower part around the heel of the foot, then make a similar sphere from cloth; and command the sick man to bend over it with his broken foot; then tie it with a long cloth.
And let the probe be made of skin, in which there is softness, so that it can be applied to the body of the foot; and tie it as necessary.
If the break is on the outside, then it is necessary: to place one probe on top and another under the foot; and keep your hand open and straight. Then place a tourniquet as the hand is rolled; and extend it between the fingers with a ligature.
If there happens to be a break in any of the knuckles of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, equalize as necessary. Then tie with the foot; and if there are any, place a straight probe beneath it, smaller, so that the break is raised and not moved.
If the break is in the other fingers, such as the middle, index, auricle or ring finger, then equalize and tie it with the healthy digit that follows it; or tie them all along its continuation; and it is better. Or place a smaller probe on top of it, as we said for the thumb.
And consider for yourself, and after this arrangement of hot abscesses; and confront with it what is necessary when anything happens from that, according to its narrative.
On the restoration of a hip fracture.
The thighbone is broken frequently and becomes visible to the senses; because it turns towards the inner and posterior parts.
And its restoration is done by tightening a ligament above the fracture, and another ligament below it. The patient stretches himself out on his face.
Then stretch all the tendons on their respective sides according to equality; and this is when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
If, however, the fracture is near the root of the hip, then place a tendon on the side of the wing, so that extension falls upward; and another ligament below it.
And similarly if the fracture is near the knee, then place a tendon close to the knee so that extension falls towards the lower parts.
Then the doctor shapes the bone with both hands until it is returned to its natural form; and the bone unites by a good union.
Then it is necessary to place plaster and a ligature if there is no abscess in the limb. If an abscess occurs in it: then leave it for some days until the abscess settles down, and then return to your treatment.
The ligature is to be thus. It is necessary to wrap a strong bandage over the fracture, that is, a plaster; and double it twice or thrice; and there remains excess of it. Then wrap the leg until it reaches the heel at the root of the ankle.
And insert a long thread between the hip and the leg near the knee towards the lower parts; and the ends of the thread come from the upper part of both sides. Then roll over the leg and hip to remove the excess from another bandage.
Then place stars (metal studs) on the hip at the site of the fracture itself, and place one star from them on the leg bone.
Then fill in voids between the leg and hip with soft cloth, to make it equal (match) the compression bandage.
Then begin with a strong compression bandage from the middle over the site of the fracture with three or four turns. And as you extend the binding from the site of the fracture, let your compression be smaller, softer, and more relaxed.
Then direct the strings towards the two ends, which you inserted between the hip and leg. Tie three stars (metal studs) on each end. Then move with the two ends of the strings downwards until you reach the ankle. And tighten both the ends and the stars on either side, so that the binding is not dislodged from its place.
Then leave the compression in place as long as necessary to avoid rot (gangrene), abscesses, swellings and similar conditions in the limb. If something should happen from this. Therefore approach it with its resolution and correct whatever may have occurred there completely, as we have said many times.
If however, there is a splinter of bone protruding from the broken bone: then it is necessary that you level it if it is possible for you. And if not, fill in the area and remove it; care for the affected site remembering the previous instructions until it heals.
And when this hip fracture is compressed, as additional metal studs are added to it, as we have said in the help section for both arms and legs.
However, in healing this broken hip, no lame-making stiffness occurs. And if it heals without the application of another leg, then the limb on which it is must always be lame.
And know that the hip is to be bound for fifty days, or a little more, or a little less; all of this depends on the diversity of temperaments and other dispositions.
On the restoration of the fracture of the knee-joint.
Know that the fracture of the knee-joint happens rarely, but it often occurs due to rubbing.
But if a fracture should occur, then it is either a cleavage or an opening in its parts. And this may be with an ulcer, and without an ulcer. It stands upright on it entirely with sensation.
And its restoration is so that it should correspond to the parts of what was fractured with the fingers until it comes together and joins as much as possible through equality, ease, and construction.
Then apply a plaster made from rice flour or milled wheat flour with egg white; place a round stopper over it if necessary; and bind it on top with the appropriate bandage.
Then consider all the dispositions that we have mentioned in other fractures; such as hot abscesses and similar conditions; so that you confront every occurrence with what is suitable for it until it heals.
Chapter on the restoration of a fracture of the leg.
Know that in the leg there are two bones; one is large and called the tibia, the other small and called the fibula.
And it happens with both of them that the kind of fracture that occurs with two bones in the arm also occurs; and for this reason the restoration of it is similar to that of the arm and the operation is one.
If both bones are fractured at once, the leg becomes bent in all directions.
If the bone is broken more delicately, the leg turns towards the front part.
If the bone is broken coarsely, you will find it lower; and it belongs to those things which are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension, equality, and ligature of stars according to that equally.
Nevertheless, it is necessary, if the fracture of the leg is a foul one, having much setting-hay: so that the extension is smaller, and more gentle with your alluvial treatment.
In the leg indeed there is something of an operation like that in the arm: and it is, that when you tie the stars, and complete your whole operation, then take two bands from pine wood, which are used in the making of solaria [a type of sandal] which is placed under tables; and they are made from the flat parts of the hand; and choose from them those with small thickness, and neither too small nor too thin; and let their length be according to the length of the leg from the knee downwards.
Then fold over one cloth with two turns, according to its length. Place one part of the leg on one side, and another part of the leg on the other side; and let them be from the knee down to the foot.
Then tie the two bands at three places from both ends and in the middle. For when traction is forbidden, the leg inclines to the right and left, and stands firmly on both feet.
And now let a cradle be made from wood according to the length of the leg, and place the leg in it to keep it still.
It is indeed very necessary that you do that in a bone fracture when there is a wound specifically.
Then consider the leg every day, and care for it with the utmost diligence, either from swelling or inflammation, or from other things. If anything from those happens, then oppose it with what is necessary, until it heals, if God wills it.
On the Fracture of Bones in the Foot and Fingers.
The calcaneus does not have a complete fracture.
The bones of the foot sometimes have a fracture, and it happens less frequently with the fingers; it does not happen to them unless there is rubbing in many positions.
If a fracture of the bones of the foot occurs and you see that those bones have risen above one another, then place the affected foot on the ground prepared as if walking.
Then stand up and place your foot on that which is raised by those bones; then press or tread it, or hold it evenly; and keep it in its place until it returns to its original position.
Then apply the plaster and woolen cloth on top; place a small table under the arch of the foot, having two flat ends.
Then bind it with a suitable bandage when you have wrapped it with linen and whatever else is needed.
When three or four days have passed, then loosen the bandage. For you will find the bones aligned, whether they are fractured or dislocated. Therefore, the treatment for them is as you see.
If some fingers are fractured, then restore them, and align them as I have told you in the treatment of the fingers of the hand. Then place a splint along the length of your finger, and let it be wider by a little; then place this table under your foot that I told you about, and bind it with a suitable bandage.
If what is fractured consists of two or more bones, then place a splint of suitable size over all the fingers, wrapped in linen; and bind the table to the underside of the foot; and let the table stand on one side of the arch of the foot outside the middle, so that it holds it with good support.
It is necessary for you to remember, so that you do not forget, everything that I have told you concerning the other fractures from the accidents that I mentioned; and apply it to every accident as needed.
On the fracture of a woman's jaw, and the shoulder bone, and a man's thigh.
When a woman's jaw is fractured, then make her sit squarely. Then tilt her head towards the back of it slightly, and hold it back.
Then the midwife shall fill the womb of [the patient] with cloth until it is filled; and let there be within the wound as if it were a sphere.
Then the woman is to be shaken, and the back of her gradually scraped off. For that reason, the cloth comes out until it becomes before the opening of the wound as if it were a sphere, and the broken bone returns.
Then place a feather upon her back. When [the woman] wishes to stir [the matter within her womb], she shall easily remove the cloth, then stir; and return it in the same way as you first introduced it; then return to the feather first.
Let that be done for seven days or thereabouts, for the bone itself is restored.
And if [you] wish, take the bladder of an ewe, and tighten a reed tube over its opening, and insert the entire bladder into her wound. Then blow into the tube with force until the bladder inflates within the wound; for the fracture returns.
Then it shall be filled again with cloth, and remain for some days as we have said, until it heals.
And when the bone of the man's or woman's breast is broken, then administer for its restoration and for the equation described above to the bone of Ankles.
And do not hide the intestines in such close fractures, which occur less frequently, especially if there is something of study or use involved.
And understand my book much, for the fracture itself shows you in many positions of restoration and narrowing [of the wound], and understand this.
In the man's arm when broken, take the throat of a goose; and insert the arm into it. Then place a covering of cloth over the [broken] limb and wrap it, and let it be for three days or thereabouts; and it will heal if God wills.
On the restoration of a broken bone, when it is with a wound.
It is fitting for the one who suffers from a fracture accompanied by a wound, especially if it is large like the hip bone or the humerus, to hasten and call for the phlebotomist immediately: if you are informed of the conditions for bloodletting as we have stated.
If the wound is bleeding, then you should hasten for its amputation so that powder made from burnt eggshells may be applied on it; if there is no other present.
Then proceed to set the fracture on the same day and do not delay it unless an abscess occurs.
If however an abscess hot occurs, then postpone the setting of it until the ninth day, until the abscess subsides. Do not approach him on the third or fourth day, for you would cause harm to him.
If the broken bone is protruding through the skin, then it should be reduced and set with your hands with ease and minimal extension.
If the extension is not prepared and the bone cannot be set by your hands, then reduce it with this instrument. And the tool is made of iron, the length of which is seven or eight fingers, and its width according to the size of the wound. Therefore it is necessary for the physician to take three or four from them according to the amount required for the treatment of any type of fracture in every case.
It should be round and its thickness should be small so that it does not become doubled during the hour of the operation under pressure on it; it should have sharp ends with a margin at its extremities. The upper part of it should be as thick as the middle up to the lower parts.
This is its shape.
It is necessary in this operation that the end of it be placed sharp and curved upon the edge of the projecting bone; and push him with it simultaneously, until when the bone returns and becomes equal to some approximation.
Make the ends of the fracture equal on both sides.
If the ends of the fracture are delicate and do not accept the tool well, then it is necessary that you cut the edge of the bone until the tool has power over it.
If you are not able to reduce them with it as we said, then cut it with it as suitable for it from the chisels which I mentioned; or saw it with one of the saws in any way possible for you.
Then plane that which remains in the bone due to roughness and small layers.
When the bone is reduced and afterwards finds a weak place after its reduction causing a violent pain, then you know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Make that. For you give great help to the weak one when you complete the restoration which you do for the bone.
Then immerse cloth in woad dyed water (woad is a type of dye used in the Middle Ages). And properly if it is during summer. And do not place vinegar or anything containing oil over the wound, so as not to cause decay and corruption at the same place.
Then administer starwort (an herb used for healing) during the hour of completion of the restoration of the bone. And let the wound heal closed; so that you cut with forceps the hole in the joints according to the size of the wound.
And beware with all caution, not to bind the wound with the fracture. For many times foolish physicians have done this; and they caused decay over the weak ones or death, or gangrene, that is, pus. Your constriction should be soft, gentle, and divergent from other fracture constrictions.
If the wound is bad or large and there are some adverse circumstances as we have mentioned, and you find pain at the site suspended: then it is not appropriate to place stars on it. Make them inversions from hard cloth at the location of the stars, and bind it with them.
And when it is one day or two days later, and you see that the wound has begun to produce pus: then remove from above it the cloth that you put on with wine. Then administer linen, and ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds: such as the four-drugs ointment and similar ones.
It is appropriate for you to loosen the binding. Consider the wound every day at morning and evening until it consolidates and heals.
It is necessary that the limb should be prepared in such a way that the pus runs out of it easily towards the lower parts.
If many days pass and the wound does not consolidate or the pus is not expelled from it: then you will know that there are small fractures of bones in that place. Therefore, you should explore the wound with a probe. How many of those small fractures are separated, remove them and extract them. And those which are not separated and pierce the limb and cause pain, attempt to cut them and remove them in any way that is possible for you.
If a boil or abscess, that is, an ulcerous sore or another kind of corruption and putrefaction happens on the wound: then it is necessary to apply to each one of them with what is suitable for its treatment, whose treatment was mentioned in its chapter.
And from that where my speech should be stationary, and to whom you present your mind when a bone breaks, and protrudes upon the limb, such as the hip bone, and the shoulder blade, and similar large bones, it is, that you do not presume on its attraction, for often death occurs from it; indeed, leave it alone until it putrefies.
Perhaps it will fall by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Therefore, take care of the wound if it seems to you; and if not, leave it alone.
Chapter on the treatment of a knot in the tread of a fracture.
Often this knot occurs in the tread of healing for a fracture. Particularly, which is near the joints; and it becomes corrupted from this shape of the limb. And perhaps it prevents the limb from its natural function.
Therefore, consider if the knot is recent; administer medications that are astringent to it, such as aloes, olibanum, myrrh, sarcocola, acacia, and similar ones.
Thus, to receive some of these or all of them; and cook them with astringent wine, or egg white, or vinegar; and place them on the knot on a plaster, and bind them over it tightly with good thread.
And leave the binding, do not loosen it for many days. Then loosen it, and again another one, until the knot is removed.
Or bind tightly over it a flat piece of lead. For lead has the property of removing everything that is feared from the limbs.
If the knot has already turned into a stone and is bound and urgently calls for its removal; then bind it from above on its part, and cut away the excess projecting out; or file it with certain files until it is removed; and take care of it until it heals.
"Concerning treatment when a limb is restored and remains delicate, not according to its natural state.
"When a bone fracture is restored and the limb that follows remains delicate and weak, then it is not done except for many reasons. "One of the reasons is an abundance of loosening of the ligament and its tying, not as required: or due to excessive tightness of the ligaments, preventing nourishment from reaching the limb: or due to an excess of superfluous embrocation at an inappropriate time: or due to a scarcity of blood in the body of the sick person and its weakness.
"And treatment for this is to feed the sick person and make his body soft until blood increases in him. "And administer a bath. "And pour joy and gladness over him.
"Then place pitch on the limb: so that it attracts much nourishment to itself. "And apply embrocation to it with warm water until the nourishment heals and returns to its natural shape.
"Concerning treatment for fractured bones when they are restored twisted.
"When a restored limb becomes twisted after healing of the fracture, and protrusion or nodulation of the broken bone occurs, deforming the form of the limb; yet the limb does not prohibit its natural operation: then it is not necessary to listen to the person who believes that the limb should be fractured again."\]     Sed si ossis fractio eius est ad modum lenta et incompleta, tunc oportet ut frangatur denuo.
And there were many foolish physicians and healers who performed that in our land.
This operation is highly to be blamed, leading to death or great fear, as it is certainly fatal.
But if lameness and swelling are recent: then it is necessary to bathe with warm water in which have been boiled soothing herbs such as althea leaves and the root of althea, sweet clover, and similar ones. Make for him an ointment with a soothing plaster, such as well-made dyachilon.
Or take the mucilage of the root of althea, grind it with chicken fat or sesame oil; and anoint with it.
Or take ripe figs, crush them with dove droppings and similar medications that are called to decrease concretion.
And sometimes the swelling is relieved by persistent, old-fashioned, and gentle friction which is done with moist hands. Move the limb to all parts at every hour.
If rigidity has already occurred before, and it has become strong and has turned into a stone, and the need for treatment with iron has arisen: then it is necessary to cut off the superficial part of it, and to relieve the continuity of the bone; and to incise what surplus there is from the swelling or bone with fine incisors; and administer ease in it with diligence and care.
Chapter on Dislocation.
Dislocation is the separation of some joints from their place. It forbids movement and distorts the shape of the limb. And it causes pain to come upon the weak and very severe injuries.
When a dislocation occurs, it is necessary to hasten towards its restoration; it must not be delayed at all.
If it is delayed, the place becomes rotten, and perhaps with it the reduction of the dislocation.
Therefore, it is not permissible to move (the affected part), nor should it be extended during its rotting hour. Because it often causes a spasm, and painful injuries.
However, when that happens, then you should hasten to the phlebotomy of the sick person. Then it is let go until the rotting subsides.
Then anoint the limb with warm water and oil. Then reduce it gently.
And treat all the limbs with that of which the memory comes back to its place.
I myself have already arranged the chapters on dislocations, even according to what preceded in the fracture, from the top part of the body down to its lower part.
Chapter on the care of dislocations of the lower jaw.
The two Jaws scarcely dislocate unless rarely.
Dislocation of both [occurs] according to one of two modes. For, they are moved away from their places slightly, and softened somewhat. Or, they are dislocated completely, with an integral dislocation, until they soften up to the chest part, until the sick person runs out of salt and cannot hold it, nor can close his mouth, and his tongue is rolled up along with speech.
But when the dislocation is slight, then it returns by itself with a multiplicity of positions along with scarcity.
But if the dislocation is complete and integral: then it is necessary that its reduction be administered quickly, without delaying deeply; and there should be someone to hold the head of the sick person; and the doctor should insert his thumb of one hand into the root of the dislocated jaw, if the dislocation is from one side. Or, he should insert two thumbs simultaneously, if the dislocation is from both sides. And the other fingers of his hands shall be outside, as required.
And command the sick person to clean his jaws, and purify them for eating in every part. The doctor commands the jaw, and pushes the jaw, until it returns to its place.
If the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are simultaneously involved: then administer fumigation with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both becomes easy; do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
When they return and are aligned, and the mouth of the sick person is closed, and they do not soften up: then place on them small cloths of fluff made from beeswax and rose oil.
Then it shall be tied with ease using soft sutures.
And the sick man's sleep should be upon his back: and his head shall stand between two pillows, so that he does not move to the right or left: nor let him try, or be forced to chew something: rather, let food be placed before him in a gentle manner, until the pain is removed, and the jaw is bandaged: then let him eat whatever he wants, and it shall be given to him with ease.
He shall not labor upon the operation of his mouth in eating and drinking, nor speak, until the jaw is bandaged and healed.
If the reduction of the jaws becomes difficult when they dislocate at some hour and do not return to their places: then often fevers occur, and continuous vomiting: and perhaps the sick man's bowel is opened, and he may vomit bile.
Therefore, when you see this: then know that it is a fatal mishap, and he who experiences this usually dies within ten days.
Chapter on the reduction of dislocation of the neck (Furcula) and the extremity of the arm.
The neck (Furcula) is not dislocated from within the side due to its continuity with the chest. It is dislocated sometimes towards the outside, and this can be felt.
The restoration [of the affected area] is such that the sick one lies down on his back and extends his arms. Then make room with your palm-like pressure, for it will return by itself.
Then place the plaster on top of it, and sprinkle powder and bind it [the wound].
However, the ends which follow the elbow should not be dislocated, except rarely. If it is dislocated on any day: then it should be reduced and set according to what has been said, and as you have prepared [the necessary tools].
Then place the plaster on top of it, and powder and bind it. And tell the sick one to adhere to tranquility and rest until he is healed.
And with this same treatment, the arm should be reduced when it is separated even from its place [original position].
Chapter on the Reduction of Dislocation of the Arm.
Know that the arm is not dislocated unless in three ways.
One way is that it is dislocated towards the palm side to the lower part. The second one is to dislocate it towards the chest. And perhaps it is dislocated towards the upper part of the humerus; and this happens rarely.
And it is not dislocated toward the back, because of the splint. And it is not dislocated towards the front, because of nerves.
Indeed, it is mostly dislocated and goes out towards the lower part, turned like a thimble; particularly in those whose flesh is scanty. Because it comes out quickly in them, and enters quickly.
But in those whose flesh is abundant, it behaves otherwise, that is to say because it comes out with difficulty, and enters with difficulty.
And perhaps some people have a blow or an accident, and the humerus is dislocated by a hot splint, and it is believed that this is because it has already been dislocated.
Therefore, you must test it, until you are certain of its nature, and then proceed with its treatment.
A dislocation is known when it turns towards the lower part like a thimble; and distinguish between a dislocated humerus and a healthy one. For you will find a clear difference among them, and in the head of the humerus you will find a hollow, and under the thimble, when you touch the head of the humerus, it feels like an egg; and an injured person cannot bring his hand back to his ear nor move it with all its movements.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest or upwards; then you will find a clear touch, and it will not be hidden.
And this kind of dislocation has an easy reduction when it is recent, or if the child is weak.
The reduction consists in raising the hand above. Then place your thumbs under the thimble, and raise the joint with strength to its place; and the hand is raised, and it is stretched upwards. Then place her below, for he himself quickly returns.
If he does not return as we said, and there has been a dislocation from the beginning, then it is necessary that the sick man be bathed in warm water, and an embrocation that softens and soothes should be administered, just as if they decoct root of althea, fenugreek, and melilotus in water, and it should be administered.
Then the sick man shall lie on his back upon his back: and let there be placed under his spine a sphere and wool, which is equal in softness and tightness.
Then the physician shall place his heel upon the sphere, and push the head of the arm with force, and draw the hand of the sick person downward.
And if you wish, bring him back in this manner: and it is to be such that you make the present man who is taller than the sick person stand at his side, and insert his arm beneath the spine of the sick person: and push the spine upward until the sick person is suspended in the air. And another servant shall draw the hand of the sick person downward towards his belly. If the sick man is light: then it is necessary that he be suspended with something else in order to weigh him down; for the dislocation immediately returns.
And it is also brought back by another manner: and it is to be such that a long piece of wood is shaped on the ground, whose head is rounded in shape like a mitten, but not too heavy nor too delicate. Then let there be placed beneath the sick man, after they have been put on top of his head, soft pieces of cloth; and the sick man shall stand along the length of the wood.
Then extend his hand to its lower part and extend the body from another side with strength; for the joint returns to its place quickly.
If it is difficult to reduce it by all that has been said, then use this care: and there is the method of using a stick whose length is twice the size of an arm, and its width four digits, and its thickness two digits; having a round head to relieve the entrance into deep places, according to this shape.
Then tie soft cloth around the round head to avoid damaging the weak stick.
Then place it under the weak point, so that the hand or arm can be extended over the stick down to the lower part; and tie the stick to the helper and arm and end of the hand.
Then place the arm on the step of the ladder according to its width, and extend the hand downwards, and let the rest of the body hang suspended from another side. For the joint enters quickly.
When its entrance is completed in any way possible; then it is necessary to place a sphere of equal size under the weak point from wool. Then place a prepared plaster made from milled powder with balm and egg white over the entire shoulder, as it is turned above.
Then tie a suture below the weak point and roll the thread over the plaster above; and connect his hand to the neck of the same person and let it not be moved for seven days.
And it is necessary to place a light food until the limb recovers; because it is quicker in healing.
Then let him lie on a larger surface after seven or five days and rub gently with movement. If it becomes firm and does not become soft again, then he is now cured.
