If the joint dislocates frequently due to moisture that occurs or because of another cause, then it is necessary to administer a cautery with three holes, as previously stated in the chapter on cautery.
If this is done completely and the ligament is dissolved after seven days and the joint does not heal, and the plaster and binding are applied but they do not hold and fall off and it cannot be lifted up to a higher place: then you know that the nerves which are at the head of the humerus have already been cut or stretched or softened. And you know that the joint will never heal in its place.
For a dislocation that occurs towards the chest and breast or is reduced to the back, this is done with pushing and stretching with hands until it returns, and then the rest of the binding and care are administered until it heals.
If pain and slowness of movement occur after healing in the limb, then the sick person should use baths repeatedly until that pain is relieved, and it returns to its original state.
Chapter on the treatment of shoulder dislocation.
You should know that the shoulder joint dislocates with difficulty and also returns with difficulty; it can dislocate to all parts, but particularly in front or behind.
And this dislocation will not be hidden from you since it falls with sight and touch according to whatever form it is dislocated. And when you join the dislocated shoulder with a healthy one, its abnormality becomes clear to you. The joint will be hollowed out and the arm cannot be doubled nor can it touch its own humerus.
It is necessary to hurry and return the dislocation immediately before a hot abscess occurs.
If a boil occurs that is hot, its reduction becomes difficult; and perhaps it will not heal at all, especially when it is in the rear region. For every kind of dislocation, it becomes worse in every aspect, and a more violent pain often accompanies it; and many times death comes with it.
And the restoration of it, when it can be reduced, is to extend the hand with both hands; and the arm should be stretched out, and the hands of the physician should be above the elbow and below it, and he pushes the dislocation together with the fingertips of his own hands or with the root of his palm until it returns to its place.
Or if the dislocation is towards the front, then it returns in this way: by placing one hand on another until it strikes with the root of the palm the elbow that is dislocated.
If the dislocation does not obey in the reduction, then administer a powerful and strong extension; and it is to be extended by two assistants: and they should hold him also so that he is not moved during the extension.
Then the arm is turned to every side after it has been placed over the hand; a piece of cloth doubled and long or a broad bandage is wrapped around it.
And when the physician touches the dislocation with his hand, he binds it with oil; so that it will be among those things which assist in lubricity of the dislocation with ease. Then push the dislocation with a strong force until it returns.
After the reduction of it, it should be placed on a plaster containing styptic and drying with ox gall; and a decent ligature is tied around it. The arm is suspended from the neck of the sick person; and let it be for several days.
Then it is unbound. If therefore the union is solidified in its place, then loosen the ligament from it, and let it be.
And if you see the union not tight enough, then apply plaster and ligate it for some days, until it becomes firm. Then loosen it.
If however pain occurs to him after the reduction of the union, and the position is in motion: then administer humectation in bathing, and fine friction, and compression, until it heals, or place over the union ilium, that is, the fat of a ram's wool. Then tie it up, and let it be on it day and night. Then remove it, and introduce him into the bath. When he sweats, then rub the union with moderate friction. Then apply oil three times, before each bathing, until it becomes soft.
And if you wish, place over it cow dung, moistened, heated with butter, and tighten it over it.
Make that several times. It becomes soft, and returns to its former position.
Chapter on the treatment of dislocations by Albucasis.
The hand of Mahassem is often dislocated, that is, the union that is between the arm and the hand.
The reduction of its dislocation is easy. On the contrary, other unions. However, it is necessary that quickness be in the reduction of its dislocation at the hour when it is dislocated, before the place is removed; or a hot abscess occurs in it.
The reduction of its dislocation is that you place the weak hand of Mahassem on a table and stretch out the attendant's hand; and the physician places his palm over the protrusion of the union and pushes it until it returns.
It is necessary to consider if the palm has already receded towards the hand's belly: then the weak one should place the back of his hand on the table for extension and reduction. And if the dislocation is proceeding towards the back of the hand, then let his hand be placed intrinsically on the table, that is, the inner part of his hand should be placed on the table, so that the physician's hand may fall upon the same projection of the joint.
If it immediately returns, that is good; but if not, then bind it with a plaster sitting upon the abscess; and leave it alone, do not touch it again.
For if it does not bear [the pressure], nor can you perform its reduction after the days have passed, yet the joint remains twisted according to its own crookedness; and nothing harms the weak one except what softens his hand, and he cannot bind anything: then know that the nerves have been severed or rubbed.
Therefore, there is no skill in it except to bind with a cautery near to the dislocation. And perhaps it brings nothing about [the healing].
Since Almahassem does not return, then place a plaster upon him as I have said. Then bind it and let it be for five days; afterwards it is to be loosened and let go, and the hand is to be exercised.
If it is deprived of motion and something heavy happens to it: then soothe it with warm water, and occasionally rub it until it is softened.
Chapter on the Treatment of Finger Dislocations.
When a finger is dislocated from any of the parts or from the back of the palm or the belly of it: then stretch out the finger, and push the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then sustain the head of the digit, and suspend it towards the side where it is dislocated, and release it for two days.
Then extend it (the dislocated part), and hold it until it aligns with its normal position on that day.
When it becomes night, bind it according to the narrative; do not cease to loosen it during the day; exercise it with motion; and bind it at night.
Do this for some days until it heals.
And do likewise with the one in the hand if it is dislocated towards the abdomen; bind it to that part, and perform your first operation on it until it heals.
And do likewise with the one when it is dislocated in other parts.
Section on treating a spondylolisthesis of the spine.
When a man has a dislocation of the backbone or neck, complete dislocation, or many vertebrae are removed from their places: then there is no cure in it, for death approaches the weak one.
And the sign of this is that the disease of the weak one departs without will, nor can he hold it; and softening occurs in some of his limbs, or two legs, or two arms, or one of them, or a vertebra is removed from its place.
For it is often removed, for it often happens that they are removed to the four parts.
Therefore, what is removed towards the backside, is named 'Gibbosity'.
But the cure of it is that you consider whether 'Gibbosity' occurs from childhood; then there is no cure in it, nor is any healing from it at all.
But in that which occurs from an accident, or blow, or similar things, the ancients have said the methods of healing in a long discourse. For which very few return with a favorable outcome.
I myself have already abbreviated from that one, for which he makes little exception due to my proximity; and in my explanation, I presented it differently than how they showed it, and explained it.
Therefore, I say that there is no skill nor healing from 'Gibbosity' which occurs from the front part.
And similarly with one which occurs on both sides as well.
And it is not cured from those except the one which occurs in the back truly, as I say; and it is to be extended on his face over a platform almost equal to the wall; and to spread beneath him moist straw so that his chest may not be hurt.
Then place a straight stick fixed in the ground at the foot-end, around its head; and another stick around its other foot-end; and let a servant hold the stick, and it should not be fastened in the ground. And let another servant hold the other (one).
Then cover the head of the sick (man), and beneath his chin tie a tight bandage; extend the end of the bandage to the stick that is at his head, and bind it there.
Then tie another bandage above his ankles, and above his knees, and at the joints (of his legs). Then gather all the bandages together, and bind them to the other stick that is at his feet.
Then all the servants should extend the bandage to any part of the stick, and neither of the sticks should be moved from their positions where they are fixed; but they should not be fastened, as we said before.
And the doctor shall place his hands on the pelvis firmly until he returns; or he may place a board over it. Then tie his feet together on the board until he returns.
If he does not return after this treatment, then take a board whose length is about three arms. And carve a hole in the wall which we mentioned near the sick (man), into which the end of the board enters. Then place the middle of the board on the pelvis. And let the doctor place his feet on the other end (of the board). And tie it firmly until the pelvis is dislocated and he returns to his place.
Then, if you wish, work with the drill that moves by hand; and it is for you to carve two sticks into the ground at the head of the sick (man) on the last foot, whose length is an arm; and at his head on the last foot, two sticks.
And let the length be that which is between every stick and a palm's breadth; and now, a hole has been made everywhere where a tourniquet runs; and let all the sticks be firmly fixed to the ground very much, so that it does not move at all.
A round stick is to be inserted; and there is a tourniquet in which a ligament is rolled in the holes of two sticks.
And at its end there is a hole, where the stick is fixed, whose length is a palm with that which is rolled; and likewise in two other sticks.
Then tie the ligaments which you tied on the chest of the sick person to the tourniquet that is at his head; and tie the ligaments which you tied on his legs to the tourniquet that is at his feet.
Then let the attendant stand by each tourniquet who will turn his hand with a cloth, with which the tourniquet is turned; and the surgeon ties the ligaments (Spondile) as we said before.
This is the form of the tourniquet that follows.
Then after Spondiles returns and the place is tied, then it is necessary that you put on an asafoetida plaster with egg white. Then starch (stuppa). Then place a star-shaped piece of wood on top of the plaster, whose width is three fingers or about that. And its length is the amount that will contain the hump (Gibbositas), and above the healthy part of Spondiles.
Then it is tied with the ligament we said before. The sick person should be nourished with delicate food until he is healed.
If something remains as a protuberance in the place at the end of healing: then it is necessary to administer medicines that soften and soothe, with the use of the tablets (tablet) that we said before for a long time. And now, it is administered on that sheet of lead.
When it happens protrusion at the last vertebra of the back: and it is thought to be dislocation, and that bone is what now projects out. Therefore, it is not fitting for this medicine to be assumed without caution, perhaps it will cause death.
Chapter on the treatment of a dislocated ankle.
Know that the joints of the ankle and shoulder do not occur unless it is a dislocation; nor does what happens to other joints by slight removal and concavity occur in them.
Indeed, the ankle joint is dislocated in four parts. This is because it is dislocated forward and backward.
Most certainly, it is dislocated more towards the inside; and less towards the front and back.
And the treatment of its dislocation towards the inside is that when you join the weak leg to a healthy one, it is longer; and the knee of the weak one projects more than the healthy one, and the weak one cannot double his foot at the knee; and there is a place following the knee which is pressed by a warm compress, because the head of the hip has already reached that place.
And the sign of its dislocation towards the outside is that there are symptoms opposite to these symptoms.
And the sign of its dislocation towards the front is that it extends the healthy leg abnormally; however, it does not double it without pain in the knee; and if it tries to walk on it, it cannot do so at the front; and urine retention occurs in it, and its knee is swollen, and during walking it is struck at the heel.
And the sign of its dislocation towards the back is that it does not extend the knee, nor can it double it before the thigh is doubled; and the leg is even shorter than the other; and the knee is softened, and the head of the hip is at that place.
Reduction of this dislocation is to consider then, if the dislocation is old, which has already become ancient with it. If you administer the reduction and it remains according to its position, then there is no cure at all in it. Therefore, it should not be presumed upon her completely.
In him whose dislocation is recent, and it belongs to one of the four kinds of dislocations: then proceed to this, that you desire a joint, and extend it to the interior and exterior, and move it right and left: forcibly, it may return, and it does not require other care.
If this does not return in this manner, then it is necessary to prepare a strong assistant and extend his leg from the lower part; either with his hands, or with a bandage that ties above his knee; and another assistant extends his hand upwards, so that he introduces his hands into the joints. Then tie a soft bandage above the root of the hip, and hold the end of another bandage.
And the extension is either from the front part of the groin to the part of the neck, or from the back to the backbone.
And let the extension be one for all, until the weak one is lifted with his body from the ground and remains hanging.
And this is indeed the common type of extension among the four kinds.
If the dislocation returns with what I said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to do all that I will say about its own treatment.
In the reduction of his [the dislocation], when it is to the insides, it is necessary for the weak one to lie on his healthy side. Then put a bandage above the root of the hip, in that which is between the head of the hip and the place under the groin. Then extend the ligament upward from the groin area to the higher part of the body, to the neck region.
Then let another strong servant take hold of his arm, and he shall reduce the large opening of the weak hip, and stretch it out by vigorous extension to the outside. Now indeed it returns to its place.
And this form of treatment is easier than the other forms, by which this limb is reduced.
If however it is excused over you, and does not obey you for the entrance with this form of treatment at all: then it is necessary that two weak feet are tied together with the strong ligament, loosely, on top of both heels, and on top of both knees: and let the length of each be four times the size of a finger: and let the weak leg be stretched out more than twice the size of two fingers.
Then the weak one is suspended above the head post (which should be in the house): and let its length from the ground be two forearms.
Then command a strong youth to brace the head of the hip: and suspend another youth with the weak one: and another youth shall push, bracing the hip with strength, for the joining (of the bones) returns to its place quickly.
In the reduction of its own (the hip), when there is a dislocation towards the outside, it is necessary that the weak one lies on a flat surface, as we have said in the case with a hump. And the ligament shall be tied above the leg of the weak one, and above its chest.
Then place two poles, one at his feet, and another at his head. Then place a third pole in the middle of the bed, firmly fixed: upon which wet cloths shall be rolled, so as not to hurt the weak one: and let the pole be between his hips, so as not to be pulled towards the lower parts during extension.
Then let every servant go to his part, and the doctor adjusts the reduction with his hand.
If he submits to the reduction, it will be good.
And if not, place it on the table and press it evenly, as we said in swellings, equally. However, it is necessary that the sick one lies on his healthy side.
And when the dislocation is towards the front, then it is necessary to quickly extend the sick person's leg: and this is according to the same story on the table: and let the doctor place the palm of his right hand on the groin of the sick one. Then bend him with your other hand, and with this bending place him extended to the lower parts towards the knee.
And if the dislocation is towards the back: then it is not necessary to extend the sick one towards the lower parts and be raised from the ground: rather it is necessary for him to be placed on a hard surface, as it should also be done if his ankle is dislocated towards the outside, according to what we said about his position on two ankles: and it is on his face and extended ligaments: according to what we said recently.
And it is necessary that pressure is applied with a table also to the place where the connection departs, when completion of reduction is done according to what is necessary.
And the sign of his reduction is not hidden from you: and it is to extend two legs of the sick one: and when you see them even, and the sick one pulls his leg, and extends himself without resistance: then you will know that the limb has returned, according to what is necessary: then join the two pelvises, and apply a plaster, and tie it with bandages, that is, a wheel: so that the ankle does not move in any direction: and let the sick one rest for three or four days.
Then loosen the ligament, and apply the plaster, and compare the leg with the other: then if you see them equal in condition, then you know that the dislocation is already fixed: therefore leave the patient to walk.
If you see something softening: then return, and apply the plaster, and bind it according to your initial binding: and let it remain for three days.
Then loosen it and make him walk slowly on both for some days until he recovers well.
Part of a Treatise on Knee Dislocations.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated towards the outer side, towards the inner side, and towards the lower side, that is, towards the back.
And it is not dislocated towards the front.
The sign of this dislocation is that you instruct the patient to apply his leg to his hip. If however it does not adhere to the hip: then you know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all modes of this dislocation is that you make the patient sit straight, so that he can now extend his leg if possible over it, and let his back rest against his face, and place his foot between your feet.
Then make the knee adhere with your palms, and straighten them between your fingers over the knee. Then apply both sides of the knee with your palm with force. And another servant extends his foot until the knee returns to its place.
The sign of its return is that the leg adheres with the hip gently, not laboriously.
Then apply it (the plaster), and make the leg adhere with the hip. Then bind them together with lint for three or four days. Then loosen it (the suture or bandage).
And let not the sick person walk for some days until he is healed.
If he refuses to be reduced (reduced swelling), then administer a strong extension with ligatures as described in the treatment of hernia, until it returns.
Â§ On the Treatment of Dislocated Heel Bone (Calcaneus).
The heel bone is sometimes displaced slightly and at other times it is completely dislocated. Its dislocation may occur towards the inside or towards the outside (inward or outward).
The sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel bone swollen, protruding towards the side to which it is dislocated.
Its treatment (for reduction) is easy; the procedure is to extend it with ease using hands and align it until it returns to its place.
When the heel bone is completely dislocated, it is necessary to make the sick person sit upright; and let a strong assistant hold him firmly from behind, in the middle of his back.
Then hold the foot with your right hand from above and the foot with your left hand below, at the level of the ankle (talus).
Then draw the foot to you with your right hand. Then with your left hand take hold of it by two joints.
Then stretch it out with your left hand and push the shinbone of the foot towards you with your right hand without effort.
Do this twice as we have said.
Then push the shinbone of the foot against the leg joint for the third time and you pull on the ankle.
If therefore it returns to its former position either once or twice according to this method, and you see that the foot is equalized, then it is good. And if not, then repeat the procedure on him, because he will return (to normal).
If the reduction is prevented by him while we are doing it as we have said, then make the weak person lie on his back on the ground and fix a peg firmly in the ground, so that he falls between his hips. And now you have wound a cloth around him to prevent him from being hurt (by the fall).
Then let the attendant take hold of his hip. Afterwards, let another attendant stretch out the leg, either with his hands or with a strap that ties around the ankle. Then all the attendants should stretch out towards the extension (of the leg). And let the peg be standing between the hips of the weak person, holding him so that his body is not drawn downward toward the extension.
Then the doctor equalizes the dislocation with his hands, and another attendant holds the healthy leg towards the lower part. For the dislocation returns quickly.
Whenever the dislocation returns and you are told of the health of its reduction, then apply an ointment and a poultice, tie them firmly with bandages, and bind the foot to the leg with a strap.
And you must be careful and take care not to bind too tightly the nerves that are above the heel at the back, so as not to injure them.
Then let him be released for two or three days. If the ligament becomes soft, tighten it; then loosen it on the third or fourth day; and forbid the weak from walking for forty days. However, if he tries to walk before that time, there is no safety unless it is loosened over him again due to dislocation, and it will be ruined, and it will not receive healing afterwards.
If a hot boil occurs to him: then it is necessary that you administer to him the recipe mentioned earlier in another place for treatment and embrocation until it is removed.
Chapter on dislocation of fingers and cure.
It is necessary that what is dislocated be adjusted with little extension without effort; and indeed this is easy, rather it is simple.
If dislocation is in some nodules of the foot heel, then it is necessary that the sick person makes his foot sit on a level with the ground or table, and stands as if walking.
Then you stand, and place your foot upon that which projects from those joints. Then press it with your foot with force until it returns and you see it adjusted and no longer projecting from its place.
Then put a board under the sole of his foot which can hold the whole foot, with two feet on it. Then bind it with a bandage suitable for three days. Then loosen it and let him rest from walking many days until it is bound and secured against dislocation.
On the types of dislocation with wound or fracture.
When something happens to it, and you try to cure it and restore it, death often occurs many times; therefore, one should not presume on its cure unless he is experienced in the art, compassionate, devout, patient, cautious, not bold, nor presumptuous: and that he uses only cooling medicines at first: and that he leaves the sick to divine judgment. Unless you see or hope for healing from destruction with gentleness of illness, and where there is something that suggests hope. Therefore, try the reduction immediately at the beginning, before a hot abscess occurs.
If the limb returns as we desire, then administer a regime that checks an abscess: and care for the wound with what is suitable for it from dry ointments.
If dislocation occurs along with a fracture, and separated fragments of the socket occur, then try to remove them, and do as we said in simple diseases in those places.
And be careful according to your ability, and guard yourself from entering on the road of fear: according to what I have previously said to you.
For it is better for you before your Creator, and more enduring for your glory, and more beneficial to your body among them in your time.
